            <meta name="dc.title" content="SMARCB1/INI1-deficient tumors of adulthood">
            <meta name="dc.description" content="The SMARCB1/INI1 gene was first discovered in the mid-1990&rsquo;s, and since then it has been revealed that loss of function mutations in this gene result in aggressive rhabdoid tumors. Recently, the term &ldquo;rhabdoid tumor&rdquo; has become synonymous with decreased SMARCB1/INI1 expression. When genetic aberrations in the SMARCB1/INI1 gene occur, the result can cause reduced, complete loss, and mosaic expression. Although SMARCB1/INI1-deficient tumors are predominantly sarcomas, this is a diverse group of tumors with mixed phenotypes, which can often make the diagnosis challenging. Prognosis for these aggressive tumors is often poor. Moreover, refractory and relapsing progressive disease is common. As a result, accurate and timely diagnosis is imperative. Despite the SMARCB1/INI1 gene itself and its implications in tumorigenesis being discovered over two decades ago, there is a paucity of rhabdoid tumor cases reported in the literature that detail SMARCB1/INI1 expression. Much work remains if we hope to provide additional therapeutic strategies for patients with aggressive SMARCB1/INI1-deficient tumors.">

            <meta name="citation_abstract" content="The SMARCB1/INI1 gene was first discovered in the mid-1990&rsquo;s, and since then it has been revealed that loss of function mutations in this gene result in aggressive rhabdoid tumors. Recently, the term &ldquo;rhabdoid tumor&rdquo; has become synonymous with decreased SMARCB1/INI1 expression. When genetic aberrations in the SMARCB1/INI1 gene occur, the result can cause reduced, complete loss, and mosaic expression. Although SMARCB1/INI1-deficient tumors are predominantly sarcomas, this is a diverse group of tumors with mixed phenotypes, which can often make the diagnosis challenging. Prognosis for these aggressive tumors is often poor. Moreover, refractory and relapsing progressive disease is common. As a result, accurate and timely diagnosis is imperative. Despite the SMARCB1/INI1 gene itself and its implications in tumorigenesis being discovered over two decades ago, there is a paucity of rhabdoid tumor cases reported in the literature that detail SMARCB1/INI1 expression. Much work remains if we hope to provide additional therapeutic strategies for patients with aggressive SMARCB1/INI1-deficient tumors.">
            <meta name="citation_description" content="The SMARCB1/INI1 gene was first discovered in the mid-1990&rsquo;s, and since then it has been revealed that loss of function mutations in this gene result in aggressive rhabdoid tumors. Recently, the term &ldquo;rhabdoid tumor&rdquo; has become synonymous with decreased SMARCB1/INI1 expression. When genetic aberrations in the SMARCB1/INI1 gene occur, the result can cause reduced, complete loss, and mosaic expression. Although SMARCB1/INI1-deficient tumors are predominantly sarcomas, this is a diverse group of tumors with mixed phenotypes, which can often make the diagnosis challenging. Prognosis for these aggressive tumors is often poor. Moreover, refractory and relapsing progressive disease is common. As a result, accurate and timely diagnosis is imperative. Despite the SMARCB1/INI1 gene itself and its implications in tumorigenesis being discovered over two decades ago, there is a paucity of rhabdoid tumor cases reported in the literature that detail SMARCB1/INI1 expression. Much work remains if we hope to provide additional therapeutic strategies for patients with aggressive SMARCB1/INI1-deficient tumors.">

  "description": "The SMARCB1/INI1 gene was first discovered in the mid-1990&rsquo;s, and since then it has been revealed that loss of function mutations in this gene result in aggressive rhabdoid tumors. Despite the SMARCB1/INI1 gene itself and its implications in tumorigenesis being discovered over two decades ago, there is a paucity of rhabdoid tumor cases reported in the literature that detail SMARCB1/INI1 expression. Much work remains if we hope to provide additional therapeutic strategies for patients with aggressive SMARCB1/INI1-deficient tumors."

class=article-subtitle></div> <div class=f1r-article-desk> <div class="authors _mdl-layout"><span class=""><a href="mailto:naparker1031@gmail.com" title="Send email" class="cauthor Parker</span></a><a href="https://orcid.org/0000-0002-5519-7758" target=_blank id=author-orcid-0><span class=orcid-logo-for-author-list></span></a><div class="mdl-tooltip mdl-tooltip--wider" for=author-orcid-0><span class=orcid-logo-for-author-list></span> https://orcid.org/0000-0002-5519-7758</div><sup>1</sup>,&nbsp;</span><span class="">Ammar Al-Obaidi<a href="https://orcid.org/0000-0001-5946-393X" target=_blank id=author-orcid-1><span class=orcid-logo-for-author-list></span></a><div class="mdl-tooltip mdl-tooltip--wider" for=author-orcid-1><span class=orcid-logo-for-author-list></span> https://orcid.org/0000-0001-5946-393X</div><sup>1</sup>,&nbsp;</span><span class="">Jeremy M. Deutsch<sup>2</sup></span></div> </div> <div class=f1r-article-mobile> <div class="authors _mdl-layout"><span class=""><a href="mailto:naparker1031@gmail.com" title="Send email" class="cauthor research-layout"><span class='f1r-icon icon-6_email orange'></span><span>Nathaniel A. Parker</span></a><a href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-5519-7758" target=_blank id=mauthor-orcid-0><span class=orcid-logo-for-author-list></span></a><div class="mdl-tooltip mdl-tooltip--wider" for=mauthor-orcid-0><span class=orcid-logo-for-author-list></span> https://orcid.org/0000-0002-5519-7758</div><sup>1</sup>,&nbsp;</span><span class="">Ammar Al-Obaidi<a href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-5946-393X" target=_blank id=mauthor-orcid-1><span class=orcid-logo-for-author-list></span></a><div class="mdl-tooltip mdl-tooltip--wider" for=mauthor-orcid-1><span class=orcid-logo-for-author-list></span> https://orcid.org/0000-0001-5946-393X</div><sup>1</sup>,&nbsp;</span><span class="">Jeremy M. Deutsch<sup>2</sup></span></div> </div> <div class=f1r-article-mobile> <div class=article-pubinfo-mobile> PUBLISHED 30 Jun 2020 </div> </div> <span class=Z3988 title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft_id=info:doi/10.12688%2Ff1000research.24808.1"></span> <div class=f1r-article-desk> <div class="contracted-details first"> <a href="#" class="contracted-details-label author-affiliations"><span class=contracted></span>Author details</a> <a href="#" class=section-title>Author details</a> <span class="f1r-icon icon-14_more_small section-control"></span> <span class="f1r-icon icon-10_less_small section-control"></span> <div class="expanded-details affiliations is-hidden"> <sup>1</sup> University of Kansas School of Medicine, 1010 N Kansas St, Wichita, KS, 67214, USA<br/> <sup>2</sup> Cancer Center of Kansas, 818 N. Emporia #403, Wichita, KS, 67214, USA<br/> <p> <div class=margin-bottom> Nathaniel A. Parker <br/> <span>Roles: </span> Conceptualization, Methodology, Writing  Original Draft Preparation, Writing  Review & Editing </div> <div class=margin-bottom> Ammar Al-Obaidi <br/> <span>Roles: </span> Writing  Review & Editing </div> <div class=margin-bottom> Jeremy M. Deutsch <br/> <span>Roles: </span> Supervision </div> </p> </div> </div> </div> <div class=f1r-article-mobile> <div class="article-page-section-box margin-bottom-40 research-layout"> <span class=box-title> <span class="f1r-icon icon-85_peer_review"></span> OPEN PEER REVIEW </span> <div class="status-row referee-reports-container"> REVIEWER STATUS <span class=status-icons> <em>AWAITING PEER REVIEW</em> </span> </div> </div> </div> </div> <h2 class="article-headings article-page-abstract" id=anchor-abstract> <span class="f1r-article-mobile-inline abstract-heading-border"></span> Abstract </h2> <div class="article-abstract article-page-general-text-mobile research-layout"> <div class="abstract-text is-expanded"> The <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i> gene was first discovered in the mid-1990s, and since then it has been revealed that loss of function mutations in this gene result in aggressive rhabdoid tumors. Recently, the term rhabdoid tumor has become synonymous with decreased <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i> expression. When genetic aberrations in the <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i> gene occur, the result can cause reduced, complete loss, and mosaic expression. Although SMARCB1/INI1-deficient tumors are predominantly sarcomas, this is a diverse group of tumors with mixed phenotypes, which can often make the diagnosis challenging. Prognosis for these aggressive tumors is often poor. Moreover, refractory and relapsing progressive disease is common. As a result, accurate and timely diagnosis is imperative. Despite the <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i> gene itself and its implications in tumorigenesis being discovered over two decades ago, there is a paucity of rhabdoid tumor cases reported in the literature that detail <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i> expression. Much work remains if we hope to provide additional therapeutic strategies for patients with aggressive SMARCB1/INI1-deficient tumors. </div> <div class=abstract-for-mobile> <div class="margin-top-30 padding-bottom-30 research-layout is-centered"> <button class="primary orange-text white-bg bigger-text abstract-expand-button-mobile with-border show" style="display: none;"> READ ALL <span class="f1r-icon icon-14_more_small orange vmiddle big"></span> </button> <button class="primary orange-text white-bg bigger-text abstract-expand-button-mobile with-border hide"> READ LESS <span class="f1r-icon icon-10_less_small orange vmiddle big"></span> </button> </div> </div> </div> <div class=clearfix></div> <div class="article-context no-divider"> <div class="article-abstract article-page-general-text-mobile research-layout generated-article-body"> <h2 class=main-title>Keywords</h2> <p class="u-mb--0 u-pb--2"> SMARCB1, INI1, loss of function mutation, rhabdoid, sarcoma </p> </div> </div> <div class=article-information> <span class="info-separation padding-bottom"> <div id=corresponding-author-icon class="email-icon float-left"> <span class="f1r-icon icon-6_email orange"></span> <div id=corresponding-author-window class="margin-top-20 popup-window-wrapper is-hidden"> <div class="popup-window corresponding-authors-popup"> <div class=corresponding-author-container> <div class="popup-window-title small">Corresponding Author(s)</div> <div class=authors> Nathaniel A. Parker (<a href="mailto:naparker1031@gmail.com">naparker1031@gmail.com</a>) </div> </div> <div class="margin-top margin-bottom float-left"> <button id=close-popup-window class=general-white-orange-button>Close</button> </div> </div> </div> </div> <span class="icon-text float-left" data-test-id=box-corresponding-author> <b>Corresponding author:</b> Nathaniel A. SMARCB1/INI1-deficient tumors of adulthood [version 1; peer review: awaiting peer review]</span>. <i>F1000Research</i> 2020, <b>9</b>:662 (<a href="https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.24808.1" target=_blank>https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.24808.1</a>) </span> <span class=info-separation data-test-id=box-first-published><b>First published:</b> 30 Jun 2020, <b>9</b>:662 (<a href="https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.24808.1" target=_blank>https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.24808.1</a>)</span> <span class=info-separation data-test-id=box-latest-published><b>Latest published:</b> 30 Jun 2020, <b>9</b>:662 (<a href="https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.24808.1" target=_blank>https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.24808.1</a>)</span> </div> <div class=clearfix></div> <div id=article-context class=article-context> <div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d21564e178>History of the <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i> Gene</h2><p class="" id=d21564e184>SWI/SNF-related matrix-associated actin-dependent regulator of chromatin subfamily B member 1 (SMARCB1), also known as integrase interactor 1 (INI1), is a crucial component of a chromatin-remodeling protein complex. <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i> was first identified in yeast in the late 1980s<sup><a href="#ref-1">1</a></sup>. By 1994, its human homologue was isolated in fibroblast cells<sup><a href="#ref-2">2</a>,<a href="#ref-3">3</a></sup>. Subsequent molecular investigations showed this nuclear protein complex enhances DNA transcription by interactions with HIV-1 integrase<sup><a href="#ref-2">2</a></sup>. Nuclear <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i> exists ubiquitously in all normal cells, and acts as a tumor suppressor gene<sup><a href="#ref-4">4</a></sup>. It was revealed in the early 2000s by studies in mice that biallelic knockout of the <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i> gene resulted in early lethality<sup><a href="#ref-5">5</a></sup>. Mice with heterozygous loss before birth, or who had later conditional single-allele knockout after birth, of <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i> developed aggressive rhabdoid tumors<sup><a href="#ref-6">6</a><a href="#ref-8">8</a></sup>. Since its discovery, much work has revealed this chromatin-remodeling protein has crucial roles in multiple signaling pathways that function to suppress tumorigenesis and tumor growth<sup><a href="#ref-9">9</a></sup>. Although these pathways are highly complex, the development and use of targeted anti-cancer therapies has practically become ubiquitous for nearly all solid tumors. Thus, continued investigations are needed if we hope to provide additional therapeutic strategies for patients with aggressive SMARCB1/INI1-deficient tumors<sup><a href="#ref-9">9</a></sup>. </p><p class="" id=d21564e239>Interestingly, the genetic signatures of SMARCB1/INI1-deficient tumors are far from monotonous. Three distinct patterns of abnormal <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i> gene expression have been identified  reduced, complete loss, and mosaic<sup><a href="#ref-9">9</a></sup>.</p></div><div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d21564e252>Epidemiology, clinical, prognosis</h2><p class="" id=d21564e255>Complete loss of <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i> expression has been linked to a number of pediatric and adult sarcomas (<a href="#T1">Table 1</a>). Malignant rhabdoid tumor (MRT) and epithelioid sarcoma (ES) both result from biallelic deletions or mutations causing a complete loss of <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i> expression<sup><a href="#ref-41">41</a></sup>. Commonly arising before the age of three years old, MRTs are considered one of the most aggressive childhood neoplasms associated with high mortality<sup><a href="#ref-41">41</a></sup>. MRTs have been reported in adults<sup><a href="#ref-42">42</a><a href="#ref-49">49</a></sup>. Based on MRT of adulthood being primarily reported anecdotally, estimated rates of incidence remain unclear. Data concerning the 5-year survival rate for MRT in adults is difficult to determine as well, as various percentages have been reported in literature<sup><a href="#ref-13">13</a>,<a href="#ref-14">14</a></sup>. However, estimated average survival following MRT diagnosis has been reported to be six months<sup><a href="#ref-10">10</a></sup>.</p><a name=T1 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 1. Epidemiologic, selected clinical, and prognostic data for SMARCB1/INI1-deficient tumors.</h3><p id=d21564e304>STS, soft tissue sarcomas; MRT, malignant rhabdoid tumor; MPNST, malignant peripheral nerve sheath tumor; NF-1, neurofibromatosis type 1; NF-2; neurofibromatosis type 2, GI, gastrointestinal; NA, data not available.</p></div><table xmlns:ali="http://www.niso.org/schemas/ali/1.0/" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" class=article-table frame=below><a name=d21564e308 class=n-a></a><thead><a name=d21564e310 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d21564e312 class=n-a></a><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e314 class=n-a></a></th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e316 class=n-a></a></th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e318 class=n-a></a>Epidemiology, Clinical</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e321 class=n-a></a>Survival</th></tr></thead><tbody><a name=d21564e326 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d21564e328 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e330 class=n-a></a>Reduced<br class=br>expression</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e335 class=n-a></a>Synovial sarcoma</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e338 class=n-a></a>5  10% of all STS; poorly<br class=br>characterized<sup><a href="#ref-29">29</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e576 class=n-a></a>NA</td></tr><tr><a name=d21564e580 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e582 class=n-a></a>Sinonasal basaloid<br class=br>carcinoma</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e587 class=n-a></a>&lt; 5% of all head/neck cancers; 0.5 cases per<br class=br>100,000 population per year; males<sup><a href="#ref-30">30</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e595 class=n-a></a>Median overall survival<br class=br>17 months<sup><a href="#ref-31">31</a></sup></td></tr><tr><a name=d21564e605 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e607 class=n-a></a>Rhabdoid carcinoma of<br class=br>the gastrointestinal tract</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e612 class=n-a></a>About 0.1% of all gastric cancers; &lt; 50 cases<br class=br>reported in the upper and lower GI tract<sup><a href="#ref-32">32</a>,<a href="#ref-33">33</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e623 class=n-a></a>Overall survival six<br class=br>months<sup><a href="#ref-33">33</a></sup></td></tr><tr><a name=d21564e632 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=3 valign=middle><a name=d21564e634 class=n-a></a>Mosaic<br class=br>expression</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e639 class=n-a></a>Schwannomatosis</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e642 class=n-a></a>Third major form of neurofibromatosis; distinct<br class=br>from NF-1 and NF-2; median age of 40 years; 20%<br class=br>familial<sup><a href="#ref-34">34</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e652 class=n-a></a>NA</td></tr><tr><a name=d21564e656 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e658 class=n-a></a>Gastrointestinal stromal<br class=br>tumor</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e663 class=n-a></a>5% of all STS, 80% of all mesenchymal GI tract<br class=br>tumors; median age of 60 years<sup><a href="#ref-35">35</a><a href="#ref-37">37</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e674 class=n-a></a>5-year, 83%<sup><a href="#ref-38">38</a></sup></td></tr><tr><a name=d21564e681 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e683 class=n-a></a>Ossifying fibromyxoid<br class=br>tumor</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e688 class=n-a></a>Only 300 cases reported worldwide; median age of<br class=br>50 years; males<sup><a href="#ref-39">39</a>,<a href="#ref-40">40</a></sup></td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e699 class=n-a></a>NA</td></tr></tbody></table></div><p class="" id=d21564e706>ES is now categorized into two subgroups: distal and proximal. Conventional or distal-type ES tends to be histologically similar squamous cells. Also, distal-type ES immunohistochemical (IHC) profiles can be diverse. Proximal-type ES is thought to be the more aggressive variant, and has an affinity for the proximal limbs of young adults. Microscopically, sheets of large rhabdoid tumor cells are predominantly observed<sup><a href="#ref-50">50</a></sup>. Based on more recent clinicopathologic and IHC data, many tumors that were previously diagnosed as a MRT are now classified as proximal ES<sup><a href="#ref-51">51</a></sup>.</p><p class="" id=d21564e717>In addition to ES, atypical teratoid/rhabdoid tumor, renal medullary carcinoma, and pediatric chordoma are rare sarcomas that result from the complete loss of <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i> expression (<a href="#T1">Table 1</a>). They predominantly occur in pediatric or young adult patients. Collectively, these neoplasms typically develop in the head/neck, CNS, thorax, kidneys, other visceral organs, retroperitoneum, trunk, and extremities<sup><a href="#ref-12">12</a>,<a href="#ref-17">17</a>,<a href="#ref-19">19</a>,<a href="#ref-26">26</a>,<a href="#ref-52">52</a></sup>. Exceedingly rare SMARCB1/INI1-deficient tumors that occur more commonly in adults include synovial sarcomas, epithelioid malignant peripheral nerve sheath tumor, myoepithelial carcinoma, extraskeletal myxoid chondrosarcoma, chordoma, schwannomatosis, gastrointestinal stromal tumors (GIST), and ossifying fibromyxoid tumor (<a href="#T1">Table 1</a>). On light microscopy, these sarcomatous neoplasms exist on a morphological spectrum. Tissue specimens are often composed of epithelioid or rhabdoid cells<sup><a href="#ref-53">53</a></sup>. However, other morphologic patterns have been described<sup><a href="#ref-50">50</a></sup>. Thus, the diagnosis of SMARCB1/INI1-deficient tumors can be difficult based on their polyphenotypic variation<sup><a href="#ref-4">4</a></sup>. SMARCB1/INI1 immunostaining can be used to confirm the diagnosis of an epithelioid or rhabdoid sarcoma because loss of <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i> expression is rarely observed in other tumor types<sup><a href="#ref-54">54</a>,<a href="#ref-55">55</a></sup>. Thus, in the absence of this genetic alteration, other malignant soft tissue tumors with epithelioid-like morphologies can be more confidently ruled out, such as melanoma, rhabdomyosarcoma, and undifferentiated carcinoma.</p><p class="" id=d21564e769>Aside from SMARCB1/INI1-deficient tumors sharing an aberration in the same gene, the relationship between these malignancies remains unclear. Following diagnosis in any age or organ, nearly all SMARCB1/INI1-deficient malignancies characteristically follow an aggressive clinical pattern and prognosis if often poor (<a href="#T1">Table 1</a>). Survival rates are often reportedly low, but they may not be accurate given low rates of incidence, and considerations for newer treatments. Also, survival can be highly dependent on surgical intervention and completeness of tumor resection, especially for chordomas. GIST are the most common sarcomas of the gastrointestinal (GI) tract. They commonly develop in the sixth decade of life and have no gender predominance<sup><a href="#ref-35">35</a></sup>. Following the diagnosis of a GIST, survival rates are highly variable and depend on specific biologic characteristics of the tumor, the type of treatment, and the risk of post-treatment recurrence<sup><a href="#ref-36">36</a></sup>. </p></div><div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d21564e786>Challenges in retrospective data collection for adult cases of SMARCB1/INI-deficient tumors</h2><p class="" id=d21564e789>Recently, the term rhabdoid tumor has become synonymous with tumors that harbor loss of function mutations in the <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i> gene<sup><a href="#ref-56">56</a></sup>. We reviewed the literature and found a paucity of cases reporting <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i> genetic aberrations in adult patients with sarcomas. A total of 450 cases of rare sarcomas were found to be described in single case reports, case series, or systematic reviews published between the years 2000  2020 (<a href="#T2">Table 2</a>)<sup><a href="#ref-57">57</a><a href="#ref-92">92</a></sup>. This number is likely far lower than the actual accounts of reported sarcoma cases in the literature. However, reports were excluded if it was apparent the case did not meet our inclusion criteria based on the publicly-available title or abstract information. Despite the <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i> gene being discovered in the mid-1990s, the majority of previous reports were excluded for not mentioning the tumors SMARCB1/INI1-deficiency status. Also, tumor occurrence in the pediatric patient population accounted for multiple exclusions.</p><a name=T2 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 2. Excluded rare sarcomas in adults reported in single case reports, case series, or systematic reviews, 20002020.</h3><p id=d21564e826>Exclusion criteria was as follows: 1.) individual patient age could not be confirmed; 2.) pediatric study population (less than 18 years of age); 3.) absence of documentation noting the loss of <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i> expression by immunohistochemistry or genetic studies; 4.) intact <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i> expression by immunochemistry or genetic studies; and 5.) non-sarcomatous histologic tumor type. PMID, PubMed Central  unique article identifier; GU, genitourinary; PNS, peripheral nervous system; GI, gastrointestinal.</p></div><table xmlns:ali="http://www.niso.org/schemas/ali/1.0/" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" class=article-table frame=below><a name=d21564e836 class=n-a></a><thead><a name=d21564e838 class=n-a></a><tr><a name=d21564e840 class=n-a></a><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d21564e842 class=n-a></a>Article</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d21564e845 class=n-a></a>PMID</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e848 class=n-a></a>Cases,<br class=br>no.</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e853 class=n-a></a>Tumor<br class=br>site</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e858 class=n-a></a>Exclusion<br class=br>reason</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d21564e864 class=n-a></a>Article</th><th align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=middle><a name=d21564e867 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e1824 class=n-a></a></td></tr></tbody></table></div><p class="" id=d21564e1830>We located 25 cases of adult SMARCB1/INI1-deficient sarcomas that were described in 18 reports (<a href="#T3">Table 3</a>)<sup><a href="#ref-42">42</a>,<a href="#ref-50">50</a>,<a href="#ref-93">93</a><a href="#ref-108">108</a></sup>. Median age at the time of diagnosis was 36 years old. A male predominance was mildly observed (14 cases, 56%), which is consistent with other larger reviews. Presentation in the head and neck (e.g. brain, eye, nose, and scalp) occurred more frequently (6 cases, 24%). No descriptive data analysis was performed to determine if our observations were significant. The majority of reports were originally described as proximal epithelioid sarcoma, but overall these remained a morphologically diverse group of cases that also included rhabdoid and mixed phenotypes.</p><a name=T3 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 3. rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e2613 class=n-a></a>5</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e2616 class=n-a></a>66 F</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 name=d21564e2626 class=n-a></a>epithelioid, rhabdoid</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d21564e2636>Treatment</h2><p class="" id=d21564e2639>Prior to, and still after, the discovery that SMARCB1/INI1-deficient tumors contribute to the large majority of soft tissue sarcomas, systemic cytotoxic agents have been used to treat this diverse group of neoplasms. Doxorubicin and ifosfamide have remained the mainstay of first-line treatment for advanced disease for the last few decades. Currently, the most widely used regimen for soft tissue sarcomas is termed AIM, which includes doxorubicin plus ifosfamide and mesna<sup><a href="#ref-109">109</a><a href="#ref-111">111</a></sup>. Therapies such as these, and other cytotoxic agents, exhibit intermediate to improved anti-cancer activity, and prolong survival in metastatic soft tissue sarcoma (<a href="#T4">Table 4</a>). However, refractory or progressive disease can occur. With the hopes of improving outcomes in patients who develop aggressive sarcomas, multiple new therapies are being introduced. Olaratumab, a monoclonal antibody that targets platelet-derived growth factor alpha and beta (PDGFRA/B), has been approved for first-line therapy in combination with doxorubicin due to improved progression and overall survival in sarcoma patients<sup><a href="#ref-112">112</a></sup>. The use of tyrosine kinase-inhibitors (TKIs) has transformed the treatment of advanced GIST. Imatinib, a TKI, as monotherapy is now approved for upfront treatment of metastatic GIST due to improved side effect profiles and outcomes in these patients<sup><a href="#ref-113">113</a><a href="#ref-115">115</a></sup>. Given its mechanism of action, imatinib is also approved for first-line treatment of the fibrosarcomatous variant of dermatofibrosarcoma protuberans<sup><a href="#ref-116">116</a>,<a href="#ref-117">117</a></sup>.</p><a name=T4 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 4. Approved first-line treatments for sarcomas.</h3><p id=d21564e2680>ORR, overall response rate; PFS, progression free survival; OS, overall survival; STS, soft tissue sarcoma; GIST, gastrointestinal stromal tumor; D, doxorubicine; I, ifosfamide; P, palifosfamide; E, Evofosfamide; T, trabectedin; O, Olaratumab; G, gemcitabine; Doc, docetaxel; NA, data not available.</p></div><table valign=top><a name=d21564e2971 class=n-a></a>Imatinib</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e2974 class=n-a></a>monotherapy</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 name=d21564e2984 class=n-a></a>55</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e2987 class=n-a></a><sup><a href="#ref-113">113</a><a href="#ref-115">115</a></sup></td></tr><tr><a name=d21564e2997 class=n-a></a><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e2999 class=n-a></a>Angiosarcoma</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e3002 class=n-a></a>Paclitaxel </td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e3005 class=n-a></a>monotherapy</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e3008 class=n-a></a>NA</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e3011 class=n-a></a>4</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e3015 class=n-a></a>8</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d21564e3018 class=n-a></a><sup><a href="#ref-137">137</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table></div><p class="" id=d21564e3027>Additional TKIs have recently been introduced, with clinical trial data showing promise for their use in sarcomas. Sunitinib and regorafenib significantly improve overall survival in imatinib-resistant GIST patients<sup><a href="#ref-118">118</a></sup>. Pazopanib, a TKI that targets angiogenesis by inhibiting vascular endothelial growth factor receptor, PDGFRA/B, and KIT, has been shown to improve progression free survival in certain histologic types of sarcoma. This led to its approval for advanced, refractory non-lipomatous sarcoma<sup><a href="#ref-119">119</a>,<a href="#ref-120">120</a></sup>. Alveolar sarcomas appear to respond well to anti-angiogenetic sorafenib and cediranib<sup><a href="#ref-121">121</a>,<a href="#ref-122">122</a></sup>. In phase II studies tivozanib, which mechanism of action mimics pazopanib, exhibits promising anti-cancer activity in metastatic or nonresectable soft tissue sarcomas<sup><a href="#ref-123">123</a></sup>. </p><p class="" id=d21564e3052>Recently, much work studying the complex mechanisms involved in sarcoma tumorigenesis has revealed the potential for numerous new drug targets. Targeting mTOR by serine/threonine kinase inhibition has been widely studied. However, thus far either only equivocal or minor benefits have been shown with the administration of these agents<sup><a href="#ref-124">124</a></sup>. In contrast, phase II trial data is reassuring for the future use of palbociclib, a cyclin-dependent kinase 4 and 6 inhibitor approved in breast cancer, for liposarcoma<sup><a href="#ref-125">125</a>,<a href="#ref-126">126</a></sup>.</p><p class="" id=d21564e3067>Preliminary data from pre-clinical and phase I/II trials is encouraging for small molecule inhibitors, such as with MDM2-antagonists, histone deacetylase inhibitors, and histone methylation inhibitors<sup><a href="#ref-124">124</a></sup>. A possible breakthrough in small molecular inhibition is represented by the recent discovery of histone-lysine N-methyltransferase EZH2 upregulation in SMARCB1/INI1-deficient tumors<sup><a href="#ref-127">127</a></sup>. Given the defining characteristic of SMARCB1/INI1 deficiency in the nearly all soft tissue sarcomas, tazemetostat has emerged as a highly intriguing compound for its direct inhibition of histone-lysine N-methyltransferase EZH2<sup><a href="#ref-138">138</a>,<a href="#ref-139">139</a></sup>. Another new agent that hopes to improve outcomes for patients with these rare and aggressive SMARCB1/INI1-deficient rhabdoid sarcomas comes from the proteasome inhibitor drug class. Ixazomib selectively targets proteasomes involved in protein anabolism and cellular apoptosis, whose activity is directly enhanced by the transcription factor MYC in <i>SMARCB1/INI1</i>-deficient states. Currently, ixazomib plus gemcitabine and doxorubicin is being studied in the phase II trial setting for renal medullary carcinoma<sup><a href="#ref-140">140</a>,<a href="#ref-141">141</a></sup>.</p></div><div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d21564e3098>Data availability</h2><div class=section><a name=d21564e3101 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Underlying data</h3><p class="" id=d21564e3106>No data are associated with this article.</p></div></div><div id=article1-back class=generated-article-footer><div class=back-section><a name=d21564e3114 class=n-a></a><span class="research-layout prime-recommended-wrapper reference-heading is-hidden"><span class="f1r-icon icon-79_faculty_recommended_badge red vmiddle default-cursor"></span><span class="prime-red big">F1000 recommended</span></span><h2 class=main-title id=d22417>References</h2><div class="section ref-list"><a name=d21564e3114 class=n-a></a><ul><li><a name=ref-1 class=n-a></a><span class=label>1. </span>&nbsp;<span class=citation><a name=d21564e3121 class=n-a></a>Abrams E, Neigeborn L, Carlson M: Molecular analysis of SNF2 and SNF5, genes required for expression of glucose-repressible genes in Saccharomyces cerevisiae. <i>Mol Cell Biol.</i> 1986; You can also read all the peer review reports by <a target=_blank href="https://f1000research.com/articles/9-662/v1/pdf?article_uuid=52ca814f-5586-4ab7-b688-8d2e83c0d799">downloading the PDF</a>.</p> Consider the following examples, but note that this is not an exhaustive list: </p> <div class=heading5>Examples of 'Non-Financial Competing Interests'</div> <ol class="numbered-list no-padding"> <li class=standard-padding>Within the past 4 years, you have held joint grants, published or collaborated with any of the authors of the selected paper.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You have a close personal relationship (e.g. parent, spouse, sibling, or domestic partner) with any of the authors.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You are a close professional associate of any of the authors (e.g. scientific mentor, recent student).</li> <li class=standard-padding>You work at the same institute as any of the authors.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You hope/expect to benefit (e.g. favour or employment) as a result of your submission.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You are an Editor for the journal in which the article is published.</li> </ol> <div class="heading5 padding-top">Examples of 'Financial Competing Interests'</div> <ol class="numbered-list no-padding"> <li class=standard-padding>You expect to receive, or in the past 4 years have received, any of the following from any commercial organisation that may gain financially from your submission: a salary, fees, funding, reimbursements.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You expect to receive, or in the past 4 years have received, shared grant support or other funding with any of the authors.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You hold, or are currently applying for, any patents or significant stocks/shares relating to class=js-article-competing-interests-policy style="display: none;"> <h2 class="h2-title u-mt--0 u-pt--0">Competing Interests Policy</h2> <p> Provide sufficient details of any financial or non-financial competing interests to enable users to assess whether your comments might lead a reasonable person to question your impartiality. Consider the following examples, but note that this is not an exhaustive list: </p> <div class=heading5>Examples of 'Non-Financial Competing Interests'</div> <ol class="numbered-list no-padding"> <li class=standard-padding>Within the past 4 years, you have held joint grants, published or collaborated with any of the authors of the selected paper.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You have a close personal relationship (e.g. parent, spouse, sibling, or domestic partner) with any of the authors.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You are a close professional associate of any of the authors (e.g. scientific mentor, recent student).</li> <li class=standard-padding>You work at the same institute as any of the authors.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You hope/expect to benefit (e.g. favour or employment) as a result of your submission.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You are an Editor for the journal in which the article is published.</li> </ol> <div class="heading5 padding-top">Examples of 'Financial Competing Interests'</div> <ol class="numbered-list no-padding"> <li class=standard-padding>You expect to receive, or in the past 4 years have received, any of the following from any commercial organisation that may gain financially from your submission: a salary, fees, funding, reimbursements.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You expect to receive, or in the past 4 years have received, shared grant support or other funding with any of the authors.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You hold, or are currently applying for, any patents or significant stocks/shares relating to the subject matter of the paper you are commenting on.</li> </ol> </div> <div id=competingInterests_1 class="c-inline-editor js-inline-editor js-form_input u-bb--all u-mb--2
